Preface  
 
After my completion of the last two years of Computer Science and Engineering to the specializa-
tion of computer security, as our benefit most of our academic curriculum concerned to computer 
security, thus, due to a series of increasingly global cyber incidence and Social Engineering fraudu-
lent as result, I decided to contribute my experience gained through this education to the organiza-
tions and individuals, therefore through writing this thesis I chosen to analyze, experiment and eval-
uate risks from Social Engineering in relation to organization’s publicly available information. 
 
 
Abstract  
Many organizations and companies are daily suffering an information leakage because of Social 
Engineering attack. The organizations claim system compromising, data theft, and business 
espionage, which are an exponentially growing problem a day after day presenting this very fast 
digitalizing world we're leaving. Traditional system security technologies cannot predict directly 
insider social engineer; however, organizational information which is publicly available may play a 
significant role to make ease for social engineers’ data harvest. The unavoidable trend toward 
online business, communications, online personal data and employee’s unawareness are another 
vital factor contributing a social engineering attack to the organizations’ data. This thesis provides 
intensive research for risk analysis for organizational information in relation to social engineering. 
 
 
Acknowledgment 
As an author of this thesis, I would like to express my greatest honor to the following persons who 
contributed to this Master Thesis in Computer Science and Engineering.  
First, I would like to thank my supervisor Professor Christian D. Jensen from DTU for all his 
assistance and support without his support the result will not be the same. 
This study was assisted by a medium-sized company whose business is an international money 
transfer which contributed much to the success of this research. 
I would like to thank all employees who have been collaborative also the leadership of the 
organization. Special thanks to Ali Jama, and Nina Johansen for your facilitating the interview with 
the organization, I appreciate your assistance. 
 Finally, I would like to thank my wife Naema for her continuous helping while writing this Thesis 
report, without her support would not possible to complete the thesis, thanks. 
 
 
 
 
==PAGE:8==
Risk Analysis for Organizational Information in Relation Social  
Engineering attack   
 
Purpose  
 
Successful social engineering in particular CEO Fraud attacks, rely on information about the 
hierarchies and structures in the organizations that are being attacked. As an attacker may not have 
inside knowledge about the organization, much of this information should be obtained through 
publicly available external sources, this is known as open source intelligence gathering.  
This thesis research focuses on an analysis of the information that is made publicly available, this is 
sometimes known as the organization’s footprint - to determine the risk of successful social 
engineering, CEO Fraud attacks. In case of cyber-attack, such as CEO Spear Phishing emails, what 
factors contribute to the success of the attack, and how can an organization evaluate and 
adequately mitigate this risk?  
The project will address the following questions: 
➢ How easy is it to identify people of importance in the organization? 
➢ How easy is it to determine the hierarchy and internal structures and procedures in an 
organization? 
➢ How do organizations identify risks from social engineering? 
➢ What are existing intelligence tools for open source intelligence gathering and social 
engineering?

Risk Analysis for Organizational Information in Relation Social  
Engineering attack   
 
CHAPTER ONE 
 
 
1          Introduction  
 
 
These days social engineers use different techniques and methods to gain access to organizational 
information. Attackers launch successful attacks after gaining the trust of the normal organizational 
system users. Due to those human weaknesses, there are repeating incidences of data security 
breaches where attackers socially engineer organization’s sensitive data, such a way organizations' 
internal system is being compromised by a hacker or a hacker takes over the control of the system 
requiring ransom money to free data. In fact, the scope of a risk for an organization's sensitive 
information is growing exponentially in 2017 (Deo, 2018). 
Because of the technology flexibilities, including online file sharable tools such google doc, emails 
and the social media, organization's employees do a lot of their work both in the office and in their 
homes, but the major disadvantage of this flexibility is being a huge information on global cloud.  
In the year 2017,69% of the attacks that worldwide companies experienced are based on social 
engineering attacks and phishing-mail attacks which breach a data security of many companies and 
organizations (Greenfield, 2017). Yet, the cyber risks are their continuing presence, which also 
requires continuous research to tackle the problems in addition to ongoing attention and 
responses. 
One of the recent attack incidents followed a large-scale economic damage happened on May 12 in 
2017, a cyber-attack called Wanna Cry (IBM Corporation, 2018) which is ransomware spread all 
over the world, but majorly the attack paralyzed the health care services and hospitals in the UK 
and US. Again, on the following month of June 27 in 2017 one more cyber-attack shacked the 
Danish shipping company A.P. Moller-Maersk, that attack was also ransomware called NotPetya, 
which caused to shut down IT systems across multiple sites and business units owns to the 
company. The attack has cost for the company up to USD 300 million (Thomson, 2017) (IBM 
Corporation, 2018). 
A question that everyone may eager to get an answer is:  How attackers get access to such 
organizations or companies that are definitely protecting their systems by using a lot of 
technologies including security software such as firewall, antivirus, intrusion detector software and 
more.  
The answer to this question is one of the fundamentals of this research, in addition, we will 
research techniques and tricks uses SE to manipulate humans and benefit their weaknesses. Have 
you ever got a suspicious email (f. ex. Nigerian mail) which you cannot confirm whether it is a 
phishing mail or not? Or some suspicious web links that you cannot confirm its origin? As long you 
==PAGE:10==
Risk Analysis for Organizational Information in Relation Social  
Engineering attack   
 
read this thesis you will understand such tricks that a social engineer uses to convince his victim to 
believe that he is something he is not.  
In this research area, there is a lot of literature, articles, and surveys which focus on the broad 
problems of cyber security or similar subjects. But it is still relatively unexplored how an online 
available organizational information that practices in the social media can open doors to social 
engineer also whether an insider social engineer plays a big with regard to his role in the 
organizational structure or not. In most cases, the articles and books do not give a clear overview, 
but merely discuss case descriptions or studies. However, this thesis research will deeply analyze 
the risks of social engineer, we will also categorize the publicly available information and determine 
that an insider social engineer can cause a great damage to organizations, not only financial but also 
the organizations' image, in turn, influence the organizations' goals and continuity in the long run. 
Organizations’ employees are not only important assets but also can be the axes of evils (access of 
invaders), in fact, employees can be a high threat because the employees know the organization's 
system, technology, and it is possible for some employees to have a full trust of their boss, 
therefore, have full access to systems. A social engineer can misuse the employees or can even be 
one of them. The only way that we can mitigate a human weakness will be to offer risk awareness 
training and giving limited access to any restricted areas, for example, organizations' servers as we 
will talk in more detail through the thesis. This thesis will focus mainly on “risks of social 
engineering” specifically, risks targeting the organizations’ online available information. We will 
develop a high-level social engineering risk mitigation model. Our findings will be a benefit for any 
organization and companies in general. Organizations can use our findings as solid information for 
the organization's risk assessment in relation to social engineering, implement mitigation controls 
and they will have better knowledge about their organization's social engineering risks. 
 
1.1        Goals  
As we mentioned in the purpose section, there are a number of goals we are trying to achieve 
during this thesis research, the next table includes the goals. 
• Identify existing intelligence tools for open source intelligence gathering and social 
engineering 
• Identify people of importance in the organization 
• Learn how easy it is to determine the hierarchy and internal structures and 
procedures in an organization 
• Identify organizations risks from social engineering 
 
Table 2: Goals 
 
 
==PAGE:11==
Risk Analysis for Organizational Information in Relation Social  
Engineering attack   
 
1.2 Contributions 
 
 
This thesis research helps both organizations and individual persons to understand social engi-
neering risks. After reading the thesis you as a reader realize social engineering is not only some-
thing you can protect or defend from your organization by using advanced system security instead 
something also caused by human errors such weakness such greedy, fearful, carelessness, and un-
intentional actions. This research will present experimental case studies that show organizations 
how spear-phishing attacks are practically working, in addition you will learn how social media 
and publicly available organizational-information can help cyber-attacks in general and social en-
gineers. The research will also contribute for analyzing, assessment social engineering risks and 
the ideal solution for risk mitigation. 
 
1.3 Project Scope 
 
In this thesis report, we will only research and analyze risks from SE into organization's online 
information, we will investigate how a SE gather organization's footprint by using Open Source 
Intelligence, the techniques he uses to influence his victim, how he uses social media and the Social 
Engineering Toolkit. This research will neither investigate why or what is the reason a Social 
Engineer do such criminals nor the legal issues of the criminal act.  
 
1.4 Research Mothed 
 
Choosing a research method will simplify our data analysis, thus at this early stage it is essential to 
consider our approach to the research questions. Since our research primarily concerns data survey 
and data collection through scientific researches, books, and papers. Therefore, we prefer to 
choose a qualitative approach (Pompon, 2016) instead of quantitative approach which normally 
used numerical data analysis, thus our research method is a qualitative approach, however, we may 
also use a qualitative approach in some relative occasions. The secondary research data is based on 
my previous experiences, research and observations gained my academic study in this field.

1.5 Thesis Structure 
 
Chapters                               Description 
1 
INTRODUCTION  
 
In the first chapter, we begun with to discuss the purpose of the thesis, Introduction, 
goals of the report, the project scope and research method 
2 
ORGANIZATIONAL STRUCTURES 
 
 In chapter two, we talked about Organizational Structures, including 
Geographical, Matrix, Flat and Hierarchical, as well as Information Structure and 
Organizational Communications 
3 
SOCIAL ENGINEERING 
 
In the chapter three, we started to talk about the Social Engineering, Exploitation 
of Human Behavior, including seven of psychological triggers, after that, we 
started talking about Gathering Information, Open Source Intelligence Gathering 
(OSINT) including Maltego, Wigle and Google Map. 
4  
ORGANIZATIONAL ATTACK   
 
In the chapter five, we talked about the Organizational Footpring (website, 
technologies), Identifying Targets Within the Organization and a case study about 
LinkedIn and Facebook to experiment finding people through social media. 
5 
SOCIAL ENGINEERING TOOLKIT (SET) 
 
 In Chapter 5, we started with SET, followed by Social Engineering Attack Methods 
(Quid Pro Quo, Dumpster Diving, Phishing, Baiting, Tailgating, Vishing, Shoulder 
Surfing and Pretexting), after that CEO Fraud and finally, we started really useful 
spear-phishing case study where we discovered many techniques and tricks. 
6 
IDENTIFYING SOCIAL ENGINEERING RISKS 
 
Chapter 6 discussed how to identify risks within an organization. We started to 
talk about Identifying Social Engineering Risks in general, then a Risk Management 
Process and empirical research afterward, Choosing Empirical Research, after 
that, we started a case study with a real-life organization to analyze risks of social 
engineering and finally, we identify the risks and evaluated.  
7 
SOCIAL ENGINEERING COUNTERMEASURES AND   RISK 
MITIGATIONS 
Chapter seven is about Social Engineering Countermeasures. We started with talk about 
the Mitigation Model. A Model which on two sides, a mitigation requirement from the 
employees' side and mitigation required by the organization. The organization’s side, we 
talked about Employee SE awareness Training, Create Security Policies, Separation of 
Duties, Least Privilege, Access Control Policy, Record Destruction, Regular Updates, and 
Monitoring and Review. We talked about the staff. to Hover over the address before 
clicking, Never Click Link, Check for secure Communication (HTTPS), bookmark always, 
Never plug in Unknown USB, Use Different Passwords, Change Passwords, Never Share 
Password, Installing three web extensions and finally preventing BEC Scams.

Chapter Two 
 
 
2                 Organizational Structure 
 
In this chapter, we will talk about an organizational structure to get a picture of which kind of 
organizational information that can be publicly available for anyone and if there is any relation 
between organizational information structure and security issues. There are four organizational 
charts which are commonly used by organizations which are Geographical, Hieratical, Matrix and 
Flat. We are going to talk briefly about those organizational charts in the subsequence sections, 
however the organization we have got an interview applied geographical structure see the below 
figure 1. Let us start with geographical.  
2.1      Geographical  
 
The geographical chart design-structure, usually applies to an organization that operates a global 
interest. An organization which has different divisions over the world and most divisions have their 
own manager in that country or region they are located. 
 
2.1.1 Possibility attack from SE 
 
As the design indicates the management of the organization is decentralized. Each division operates 
in different countries, most of them have their own processes and comply their local regulations, 
that means they do not have a unique security politic and SE training programs. Especially, the 
organization we have interviewed told us that, they often use social media, including OSINT in 
general and they do not have any common cyber security program. It is very likely that a SE conduct 
information gathering using OSINT1, therefore this organizational design has more tendency to get 
an attack from a SE. The interviewed organization belongs exactly the below chart (fig1), thus, the 
risk or impact can be very high which you can see in section 6.2.6.1.1 in. next will be hierarchical 
chart. 
                                                          
1 See section  4.2 
==PAGE:14==
Risk Analysis for Organizational Information in Relation Social  
Engineering attack   
 
 
Figure 1: Geographical Organization Chart 
 
2.2 hierarchical chart 
 
The design of hierarchical structure, tasks are organized different area where the power is on the 
highest stairs. The structure has very fixed alignment and the highest guys are executive CEO, 
directors, and managers. This design is often used by military and non-profit organizations. The 
power is placed on the top leadership of the organization. The next figure 2, is a hierarchical design.  
 
2.2.1 Possibility of SE attack 
 
A SE is able to initiate collections of the organization's publicly available information using OSINT. 
He can also attack the organization; however, this design differs from the previous talked designs 
the power and decisions are more centralized. The impact and damage from a successful cyber-
attack can be very high, however, the responsible department or responsible persons can be very 
easy to discover. 
Figure 2: Hierarchical Structure Chart 
==PAGE:15==
Risk Analysis for Organizational Information in Relation Social  
Engineering attack   
 
2.3 Matrix chart 
 
Usually, the Matrix organizational structure in figure 3 is used by large organizations because, they 
have large projects, product or maybe services, therefore they need teams work for that projects, 
services, and products. So, they create a functional specialist to work on different projects, services 
or products, also several team leaders from different parts of the report. When each team finishes 
its project, they report to their department. 
 
2.3.1 Possible SE attack  
 
Where the security department is located depends on the choice of each organization, but as we 
can see on this design structure, it seems that the control and accountability are easier than the 
hierarchical design or geographical design. A SE can gather the organization's footprint from the 
organization's publicly available data (website) using OSINT, or from insider SE also a SE attack is 
probably possible, however, as we can see the alignment of the design, an attack may only affect 
one specific department instead affecting the whole organization. A damage or impact of a 
succeeded attack for organization using this design, I think would be less than the damage would 
have been if the SE-attack succeeded for organization using flat or geographical design because I 
supposed that attack is limited to only one department. 
 
 
Figure 3: Matrix Organizational Chart 
 
==PAGE:16==
Risk Analysis for Organizational Information in Relation Social  
Engineering attack   
 
2.4 Flat chart 
 
Most small organizations use Flat design and it is suited to a small organization. The Flat 
organizational structure has a limited administration that each runs a broader range as you can see 
on the figure 4. The flat design gives an employee a power. Flat structure is decentralized 
organizational structure. In terms of critical decisions, they have different employees collaborating 
each other and deciding how to tackle any significant new situation including a cyber-attack. such 
an organization has one headquarters, that controls and collaborate everything about the 
organization. 
 
2.4.1 SE attack 
 
At the side of employee satisfaction, a Flat design is much better than other organizational designs 
for flexibility’s sake. As we mentioned before, the decision is faster than other designs, however, if 
a successful SE attack occurs, then the damage is bigger than organization using other charts 
because the authority is decentralized in different places and accountability is unreliable. SE is able 
to make data gathering by using OSINT and then can attack their system. Anyway, I believe the 
impact or damage can be enormously greater than other organizational designs, because it is 
difficult to know who did what. 
 
 
The advantage of having an organizational-charts is for laying out relationships within the 
organization to ensure that everyone knows who / how to communicate with essential information. 
The information includes that communication is very important for hacker to collect before he tries 
to exploit the organizations system.  
Today most of the organizations have a website they put on their information and their power 
structure, therefore a publicly accessible organizational chart can be part of the reason to leak 
sensitive information. With the help of such information an attacker can easily recognize a 
particular person in the target company, and maybe some of the vulnerable online contact

they were talking about their follow strategy they did not mention any security issue or negative

communication methods include face-to-face, telephone, email, fax machines and mobile devices,

member of the company’s executive team is involved or internal communication that include all

types of interaction namely written and verbal messages where the communicating employees 
have same lower-level, however, a communication between a manager and a low level employee is 
both internal and executive communication, in other words, organizational communication can be 
vertical (low level employee to manager) or horizontal (communication between same level 
employees), nevertheless in our case, according to our interviewee from the organization in chapter 
six, the organization's communication is done through both a vertical and horizontal, in addition the 
organization use telephone, email, social media and rarely face-to-face meeting(more in section 
6.2.4.11), in spite of this, organizations normally follow/obey their organizational security policy 
which guides how their sensitive information should be exchanged inside or outside the 
organization. more about the security policy see in section 7.1.2.2. in chapter 7.

Chapter Three 
 
 
3          Social engineering 
 
What is a Social Engineering? A social engineering (SE) is an art of manipulating people 
(ANTONUCCI, 2017). SE is one of the most powerful tools for breaking into any computer system or 
network also most important skills that a hacker needs to have to launch out a successful social 
engineering attack. in addition, it's one of the most dangerous techniques that hackers used to hack 
into computer systems. Basically, when we're talking about social engineering in this thesis, we're 
talking about hacking into your target through the people.  
 
It doesn't matter what the target is, it could be a website belongs an organization, it could be just a 
person working on that organization or it could be a company as well. The whole idea is to use the 
people associated with that target in our case with that organization or with that Web site and then 
hack into that target through the people. As we mentioned in introduction the most of the 
organizations or companies spend a lot of money on securing their software so they have teams of 
security people and ethical hackers trying to hack into the company and making it as secure as 
possible but they often forget to educate their employees and teach them to secure themselves 
from social engineering attacks. Therefore, SE is very important techniques that organizations need 
to be able to understand to block these kinds of attacks, it doesn’t matter whether this is coming 
from an attacker on the outside or on the inside of the organization. The whole idea of social 
engineering is to hack the people, to have the web admin password. Organizations need to 
understand these types of techniques that social engineering attackers use, however during this 
thesis research we will explain a lot of them so that organizations can keep their safe, both online 
information and in the real world. 
 
3.1 Exploitation of Human Behavior 
 
Exploitation of human behavior are mistakes which people execute regardless of consequences. 
The cause of the mistakes are different methods based on human weaknesses, including lack of 
knowledge, however, we are going to talk about some of them in the next section. 
 
 
 
 
==PAGE:20==
Risk Analysis for Organizational Information in Relation Social  
Engineering attack   
 
3.1.1 Psychological Triggers behind SE 
 
The people usually have different behavior patterns to think when they have to decide on a matter 
of urgent issues. They may decide on the basis of their human nature, including culture, trust or 
carelessness, but a SE takes advantage of this human diversity behaviors to exploit his target 
through the human, thus, during a SE attack, there are psychological triggers that drive the victim to 
do what he/she may not normally to do, however, there are a number of psychological triggers, but 
we will make short briefs to those in figure 5 
  
 
3.1.1.1           Greed 
 
Attacker offer people something that they want, see my email example in section 5.1.1. Many 
people receive emails like this and certainly one of the most common exposures that a social 
engineer use in his phishing emails, in this case, the attackers prying on the greed of the victims and 
this is why we see in his offer GB 800.0000.  in section 5.1.1. email example 
The SE says, I have this money, I would like to give you some of them, I just need a little bit of 
information from you and of course the information being requested down here email, will then 
lead to further questions and ultimately the attacker is socially engineering the victim’s personal 
identifiable data out of the victim, so greed is a human weakness that makes us all vulnerable 
3.1.1.2               Strong Affect 
Strong Affect is a technique that SE can frighten or excite the victim into acting in a way that they 
wouldn't normally do. Here an attacker is relying entirely on the excitement or fear of the victim. 
Figure 5: Psychological triggers 
==PAGE:21==
Risk Analysis for Organizational Information in Relation Social  
Engineering attack   
 
For instance, a SE may saying that they have same sensitive of personal information about the 
victim and if money is not paid they're going to leak that information online which would damage 
the victim. If the victim is fearful enough about that happening. Strong Affect consisting many 
things including fear, panic, guilty and excitement. An excitement, such as the person who promises 
to earn or get a lot of money. SE tries to motivate his target emotionally. 
 
3.1.1.3                  Reciprocation 
Reciprocation is a psychological trigger that works in such a way that a SE supports a victim with the 
something like, telling a good antivirus software at the same time or afterward asking the victim to 
do a small favor (MITNICK, 2002, s. p. 414). When someone does a favor for you, it is normal for the 
normal human behavior to do same when it comes to your time, eventually, a victim accepts the 
SEs request, but unfortunately, that is the time the reciprocation gets involved, therefore acting 
reciprocation is human weakness regarding social engineering.  
 
3.1.1.4                    Overloading  
Overloading is the Psychological Triggers that takes effect when a person is overwhelmed by 
information, and does not have enough time to think (Gragg, 2002). Overloading includes anger, the 
target victim needs time to think about immediate information but not time available. 
 
3.1.1.5                   Desirable to Help 
Desirable to Help also called Diffusion of Responsibility and Moral Duty (Gragg, 2002, s. 8) is a 
psychological trigger which is activated when the victim sees the situation that he must to accept 
what SE wants to do. A SE may call or e-mail to the victim insisting he is one of his colleagues and he 
is in trouble. for example, some will shoot me if you did not give me this password or pay this 
ransom money or similar scenarios. 
 
3.1.1.6                     Authority  
Fear of authority is a psychological trigger enable the victim or a person through a target victim to 
obey a SE's order (MITNICK, 2002, s. p. 176,413). A lot of people are fearful in the presence of 
someone they sense as an authority shape. This absence of perspective leaves this trigger full open 
for exploitation by anyone likes to mislead him or herself as an authorized person. 
 
3.1.1.7                     Deceptive Relationships 
Deceptive Relationship trigger is about a relationship between a SE and an employee-member in 
order the SE to find information about the target organization that he can use to exploit another 
person in the organization, as Kavin Minick says "the social engineer uses the art of deception to 
influence the person on the other side of the door to open up for him" (MITNICK, 2002, s. page, 296). 
The SE and the employee may then build so honestly or close relationship. They normally use 
emails or phone as a communication channel (MITNICK, 2002, s. p. 75), then a SE can get wanted 
information, for example, which technologies the company use, security issues and their product, 
hus the deception relationship is human naivety or weakness that causes a SE attack. 
==PAGE:22==
Risk Analysis for Organizational Information in Relation Social  
Engineering attack   
 
3.2 Gathering information  
 
The first step that a social engineer starts before any attack is to gather information to exploit or 
compromise a system. Usually, there are two techniques used by a social engineer (SE) or attacker 
to collect information about the target. As the next figure 2 shows, we can call these two 
techniques:  
1) Gathering passive information   
2) Gathering active information. 
 
The both scenarios are collecting information, moreover the figure 2 presenting the circle of attacks 
that an attacker or SE follows when they want to launch an attack in addition, the Most of the 
material I will talk about in the following chapters include the figure 2. 
 NB: During the writing this thesis, I interchangeably use SE and hacker, knowing that a social 
engineer can be hacker when the hacker is not necessary to be SE. 
 
Figure 6: SE attack plane 
 
 
3.2.1      Passive information 
 
Passive information is any information that an attacker gathering from publicly available resources 
like the organization's website, Google, Wigle, Maltego. A SE may use searching on Google, 
searching for social media, but he/she is not interacting with the victim. During the passive 
==PAGE:23==
Risk Analysis for Organizational Information in Relation Social  
Engineering attack   
 
information gathering phase, an attacker looking for both technical information such operating 
system that a victim uses and a personal information such e-mail, name or working ours. The 
technical information an attacker can use for the different technical attacks like weakness of 
discovered server while personal information can be used for social engineering. This can be done 
technically or through people that are working victimized organization, however a SE is looking for 
all kinds of weakness. He is looking for network weakness, applications weakness people weakness, 
therefore he considers that as much information as he can collect then he can launch an effective 
social engineering attack.  
 
 
3.2.2            Active Information 
 
When it comes to gathering active information, a hacker is going to interact to the victim to get 
active information like open port services which is a logical communication channel, network 
topology vulnerability so a SE is going to get more critical information and this would be definitely 
very useful while SEs are planning for the attack. Searching active information includes using DNS 
enumeration, using ping utility to insure whether the system is live, using nmap which is very 
powerful utility, for example using  “nmap -sS -o 192.168.162.249, here “-sS” means show the open 
port and what service are using those ports to know whether that system is vulnerable to hack it 
also “ -o” show the operating system that computer is running as well the MAC address. Although 
we can use nmap in our Kali terminal, alternatively we can use zenmap which is graphical way of 
using nmap , the next figures are my network scanning, I used zenmap utility to visualize the tracing 
information, just for experimental, however it is not legal to use nmap scanning without written 
permission from the system admin or the oraganization’s CEO. 
 
Figure 8: Zenmap scanning 
Figure 7: Zenmap topologies 
==PAGE:24==
Risk Analysis for Organizational Information in Relation Social  
Engineering attack   
 
 
 
Zenmap can also show you topologies, how these computers are connected to each other and 
which machine is more vulnerable than others. The hosts, open ports, rooters and switchers are 
also displayed. All this information is active information which are useful when attacking.

3.3.1                      Introduction 
 
One of our research goal is to identify existing an existing intelligence tools for open source intelli-
gence gathering and social engineering, thus in this section we're going to talk about Open Source 
Intelligence (OSINT) which means we’re going to be looking at a lot of different ways that infor-
mation and Intelligence can be gained from various sources to aid social engineers in achieving their 
objectives.  
 
3.3.2                                 Overview   
 
When we talking about reconnaissance (MITNICK, 2002) and finding things of value to the social en-
gineer, we see this quite a lot of personal information on both organizations and people that can be 
easily discovered by using a variety of different techniques some of them we are familiar with our 
everyday life such using social media, however we're going to take a very specific social engineering 
look at how to use these services to find information that could be useful for someone with mali-
cious intent. That might sound like quite sensitive data, but much of what we are talking about is 
information that already publicly available it's just a question of knowing where to find it and what 
sort of data attribute a SE needs to look for in order to successfully socially engineer target.  It is not 
only much of this already freely available online it's also often easy to socially engineer more infor-
mation at one individual such that information can then be used in a subsequent social engineering 
attack, furthermore very often this information cascade chains certain pieces of information times 
from some individuals to obtain more from other individuals which eventually lead to exploit. The 
idea is not only talking about sources of information that we found online, but there's also human 
==PAGE:25==
Risk Analysis for Organizational Information in Relation Social  
Engineering attack   
 
based reconnaissance. Reconnaissance performed in person and again some of the techniques 
we're going to discuss in this chapter would probably already familiar to the reader, in fact you have 
probably done them yourself perhaps inadvertently or may be intentionally.   
 
 
3.3.3                            Understanding OSINT 
 
Open Source Intelligence (OSINT) is a very common term used whenever we are talking about social 
engineering (Line C. Pouchard, 2012). We continuously use to access OSINT from the web, for ex-
ample, we have probably looked at LinkedIn and identify the various roles and positions in history 
that an individual might have. That sort of intelligence and it is intelligence certainly can be used in 
an intelligence context and it is very useful.  
It is worth to mention here that OSINT has got nothing to do with Open Source Software they are 
two completely different technologies. It is however differing to signals intelligence, so OSINT is an 
intelligence gathering by interacting of signals many online sources. Let us see the list of some very 
used today's OSINT. In the subsequent section, we will classify OSINTs which we may almost very 
familiar.

Chapter Four 
 
 
4              Organizational Attack 
 
Attacking an organizational-network require careful planning including reconnaissance we have 
talked in chapter two, however there are fundamental steps that attacker may insure before the 
attack happen. These steps are not conditional, but instead simplifying the attack such a successful 
social engineering attack, however, a social engineer may launch his attack for anytime. These steps 
including understanding organizations layout, also called an organizational footprint (Boyd, 2000), 
after that scanning and enumeration steps that we talked in chapter 2. We are not repeating what 
we talked already but, in this chapter, we are going to start talking about organizational footprint in 
the next section. 
 
 
 
 
 
 
4.1.1                   Organizational foot print 
Figure 13: Organizational footprint3 
            
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
The success of Social engineer targeting an organization is very heavily determined on how much 
information they have about that organization. The more data points they can put together and 
better they can understand that target, the more likely they are to be successful in their objective, 
so building up this organizational picture is real fundamental of first step of social engineering. And 
would often begin by using some of basic OSINT sources that have talked so far. There are many 
different locations that very useful information about the target organization might be found. 
Perhaps the most obvious is their website. We are probably going to find their physical location, 
there you might find who the executives of the organization are. The high-profile people in 
positions of power they could be a very useful target for a social engineer and the products the 
organization makes. A social engineer who understands what the organization is producing is going 
having information that will make it easier for them when they need to convince their target to 
hand over information such fraud CEO. The understanding the technology an organization uses is 
another valuable piece of information, there are many different places we could discover that 
information, let us start by talking briefly an organization website

as we mentioned above, for example, knowing what server software they are running. In the 
website, they may introduce their product, locations, contact persons, work division, 
responsibilities and maybe more. After an attacker educated this information from the 
organization’s website he can also use the online OSINT technologies that we have talked in chapter 
2 to get more specific information. 
 Another sort of very useful organizational reconnaissance can be site tour if the organizations 
website provides the ability for you to come and see what they do. I have been on site tour before 
not for social engineering purposes, but because of being genuinely interested in what the 
organization does and there is quite view of big name brands we probably already very familiar with 
that offer ability to bring people inside into the premises in order to see how they operate that can 
give a social engineer quite a lot of valuable information, especially they can observe the inner 
workings of the organization. That is something that they can use when they are trying engineer 
their target within that organization to hand over information to them, this is just very few things 
we have mention so far, though there's a whole bunch of other sources of organizational 
information that we can discover, let us talk the technologies in the next section.

amount of power within the computer networks of an organization. We are going to use a case 
study to demonstrate how easy it is to find a system admin in an organization by using LinkedIn.

people of very specific roles within the very specific organization. In the next section we starting our

organizational footprint. We explain that he uses OSINT where he first explores the organization’s 
website, the technology they use and the product they sell or their suppliers, then he tries to 
interact the company by pretending himself as a person who works for the same company as the 
one the victim works to get information from the victim. We also talked about how a cyber attacker 
identifies as an important person such system administrator or CEO of the organization. Finally, we 
perform a case study to experiment how easy is for SE to use social media like LinkedIn and 
Facebook to find organization’s important persons.

5.1.1                         Phishing 
 
Phishing is the number one type of online social engineering attack both because it's the most 
prevalent and because it's one of the most successful attack (Christopher Hadnagy, 2010). Again, 
phishing is the number one attack because it's so easy to do to so many people as you will see on 
our case study experiment (Nadeem, 2015). If you've ever gotten a fishing e-mail before it looks like 
it comes from someone official like your bank or your employer or your university, or some 
authorities telling you that have won a lottery or you are among the randomly chosen winners as 
you can see in the email on figure 22 below. I received this mail from an attacker. 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
Phishing mails also looks like it is coming to you from someone that you recognize someone that 
you do business with like your eBay or your PayPal account. When it comes to fishing they are just 
trying to get your all your information including the list on the above letter and your credentials 
username and your password to hack into your account (Nadeem, 2015). Unfortunately, phishing is 
number one because it's so effective, however, isn't just something that people do because they 
are dumb, it is because we all get distracted. All victims are susceptible to the same types of social 
engineering techniques that we mention in figure 21. Phishing is just sending an email and trying to 
==PAGE:37==
Risk Analysis for Organizational Information in Relation Social  
Engineering attack   
 
get you to click through a link or to give up your information, just look at the mail figure 22. But it is 
based on some other social engineering techniques and these all work together in an effective 
attack. Let us talk about Pretexting technique.  
 
5.1.2                            Pretexting 
 
The pretexting is pretending to be someone you're not or setting up a false pretext a false situation, 
in other words pretexting just means using some false information or made up information to try to 
make a connection with the user (MITNICK, 2002). An attacker says he is calling from corporate or 
he is calling from computer support as you can see in table 7 (Christopher Hadnagy, 2010). They 
send an email looks like from a friend who is in trouble they are traveling someplace and they need 
some money. They only want to obtain a victim’s sensitive information including passwords and 
account number (Nadeem, 2015).  
There are lots of pretexts that people use both online and in the real world as a predator might say I 
am a friend of your dad's or I am a friend of your moms. We have to understand and you have to 
talk with your employees about social engineering both online and in the real world in a chat room 
so we could say that they are in your class or they're also they work for the same organization. 
I have a practical example that has happened to me. It was 15 of February in this year 2018, 
someone call to my wife’s telephone claiming he is from IT-security Danish company and he wants 
to remove a virus from her computer online but he was speaking only English. I asked my wife if I 
can talk to him. The next table 9 is our phone conversations.

5.1.3                                    Dumpster Diving 
                
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
         
 
 
 
 
 
 
             Figure 26: Dumpster diving7                            
 
 
 
 
 
 
Dumpster diving is collecting information from organizations discarded materials such as old 
computer or equipment belonged to the organization such as papers, hard drives, USB drives, 
DVDs, or CDs and company documents that were not disposed properly or securely manner (Kenin 
D. Mitnick & William L. Simon, 2005). 
 
 
5.1.4                                 Baiting 
                          
                Figure 27: Baiting USB8 
 
Baiting is like someone intentionally leaves a USB drive out in the parking or inside the 
organization’s building (Christopher Hadnagy, 2010). A lot of organization's employee and natural 
thing for people to do unfortunately is pick up that USB drive and plug it in to see who it might 
belong to or see what might be on it. Unfortunate, but the attacker puts malware on there, it may 
have just attacked your organizational network from the inside unintentionally. They didn't know 
they were doing anything wrong. But this is something important to train organizations’ employees, 
don't just pick up a USB drive or accept a free download or free software. Sometimes that click can 
take you or your organization to a website that is compromised.

5.1.5                              Quid pro quo 
 
Quid pro quo is a common social engineering technique that means you'll get a little something if 
you do something for me (noget for noget) (Nadeem, 2015). A Nigerian prince e-mail scams are a 
terrific and terrible example of this if you will help us get this money out of the country, then we 
will send you a portion of it or we'll let you keep a portion of it. More could be just something as 
simple as hey if you'll do this for me I'll send you this  
 
 
information or I'll give you some money. A quid pro quo is what makes these types of attacks just 
means this for that something for something else. I receive the next mail from someone called 
Davies  Adams who I did not know ever and he convincing me a business deal  to click the malicious 
link , se the mail. 
Another very sophisticated spear phishing, which, if you're a greedy person and you do not know SE 
techniques including spear phishing emails, you'll probably quickly responding that mail.

5.1.6                                     Tailgating 
 
Tailgating is another common attack. This can happen both online and in the real world. The most 
common way that tailgating used is for example an attacker stands outside an organizations’ 
building with some employees maybe in a smoke break area and then when they go in back into 
work after attacker struck up a conversation just follow one of them in, or he comes in holding a 
package in his hands or looks like a technician, wearing common technician dress and he waits until 
someone comes to the door (Nadeem, 2015). He walks in right behind them and it allows him to 
gain access to a building (Kenin D. Mitnick & William L. Simon, 2005). Tailgating is a real thing in real 
life it can give someone access to the organizations’ computers in their network and their physical 
locations. Physical access can mean total access, in addition if someone stranger can touch your 
computer he may be able to own your computer and your network.  
Figure 29: Phishing mail 2

Engineering attack   
 
5.1.7                                       Vishing 
 
A vishing (combination of voice and phishing) method is just a voice form of phishing. Someone 
makes a phone call and pretends to be someone that they're not company I.T. support (Nadeem, 
2015). Someone sends you a simple message service or text message pretending to be your bank 
pretending to be your employer pretend to be an attorney pretending to be your doctor. They’ll 
leave you messages or call you up on the phone and they can even fake the caller ID number on 
your telephone and make you believe that it really is a call from example taxation (SKAT) authority 
number or some other government agency. This kind of call you must be very careful not to give 
out any information. Remember the taxation authorities does not call most law enforcement 
agencies don't make phone calls. They either send certified mail if it's taxation authorities or they 
will come in person if it's law enforcement (Police).  
 
 
 
5.1.8                                    Shoulder Surfing  
 
Figure 30: Shoulder surfing9 
 
 
Shoulder surfing refers a situation w (Sikipedia, u.d.) here someone is sitting in the train with a 
laptop and a SE might be standing behind the victim and he can see exactly what is on the screen. A 
SE looks at victim’s shoulder and observing victim’s personal information and this sort of 
information that might be visible to casual reserves including passwords. When people have 
punching a pin number into the screen it's just for six or eight digits that is something that is quite 
easy for a casual (Il-Soo Jeon and Myung-Sik Kim, 2017). A SE may also see information on the 
screen that could be sensitive such as corporate document, employee records and confidential 
communications. This is physically looking over the shoulder, however a social engineer may 
observe his victims at a distance by using hidden camera or telescope.  
In terms of places at risk obviously public places are going to be high risk so a lot of people like to 
work. The public transports such as trains, buses and places where is a high density of people even 
airplanes where we are sitting for a long period of time, possibly working away. Could the social 
engineer behind you actually see what's on your screen and more information of a sensitive nature 
so shoulder surfing can be a serious risk and the ability for someone else to observe and perform 
reconnaissance on what it is that you're doing.

5.1.9                                     CEO FRAUD 
 
CEO fraud, also known as Business Email Compromise (BEC), is a type of spear phishing mail attack 
which criminals steal a million of dollars as fraudulently. The technique involves posing as an 
employee of the organization. A SE most often use the position of the CEO when attacking. A 
fraudster or cyber attacker uses an enough time to study his target organization such as 
organization’s top executive (CEO), CEO’s vacation schedule, organizations supplier and 
organizations billing system. After a SE collects all that valuable information about the victim, then 
fraudster makes sur 
e the time the victim CEO is on vacation and he is not his office. When the criminal knows all this 
information, then he contacts with a specific employee, such chief of financial officer or accountant 
and requests a large money transfer impersonating as he is an executive officer in the company. The 
next picture is an example of BEC mail. 
                 
 
 
     Figure 31: BEC email10 
According to a report from (Ed Cabrera(Chief Cybersecurity Officer), 2016) a 31% of BEC scams 
impersonated the CEO of the company , you can see in appendix C. One of the latest incidents of 
the BEC was in 2017 when scammer cheated 11.8 million from the Canadian University (Survey, 
2017). This trick succeeded for the scammer after fraudulent mails sent to the university staff 
advising them that one of their client’s account was updating their bank account information. Let us 
take one more report. According to a report made by Proofpoint, Inc, (Proofpoint Inc, 2017), when 
they were talking about a spear-phishing attacks to organizations in 2016 compared to the year of 
2017, they expressed that attacks rose sharply to 13.8% quarterly in 2017, increasing from 75% in 
2016 to 88.8% in 2017, see the graph in Appendix  E, however how to protect your organization

Before we continue these spear-phishing mails, we would like to introduce a very sophisticated 
technique to hide the IP address which makes very difficult to understand a normal user, however 
SE and cyber hackers use that tricks constantly to mask their IP address.  
 
 
5.1.10.1.3                  IP address masking  
 
We know that if we type the www.facebook.com , then our domain name system or DNS system 
that runs most of the Internet recognizes our IP number, because that domain name system 
resolves that name such as facebook.com to a web address and that web address takes us to the 
web site, in other words the DNS translates the facebook.com to IP address and vice versa, 
therefore if we ping www.facebook.com into our terminal we will find the IP number of 
facebook.com. however, what many people do not know is that you can also type the IP number 
instead of the domain name to visit for instance www.google.com , but it is always easier and 
convenience for humans to remember names instead of IP numbers. Our aim was to explain for the 
reader that either the IP number or the domain’s name can be typed to find a website in the most 
of the cases. Let us explain “@”extinction. 
 
5.1.10.1.4                    AT (@) extension 
 
One of the most serious trick used by SE or hackers is using "@" extension, like for instance  this 
way, http://ww.facebook.com@google.com. If you see like this address link, it does not mean you 
are going to Facebook.com but instead Google.com. if you look at the figure 38, you will realize that 
all the part before google.com in the link are grayed out assuming that is a username. It is like when 
we creating direct link of FTP file. 
 
 
                                                             
 
Now instead of typing this http://www.facebook.com@google.com ,  a SE or hacker replaces the 
last part "google.com" to their IP number which is intruder's hosting server, f. example, my Kali 
Linux IP number 192.168.186.143,like this one http://www.facebook.com@192.168.186.143 , and 
to make it more difficult to understand they use to convert the IP number to a long integer number 
like this one “3232283278” which is my Kali Linux  IP converted to integer number, then we can 
again rewrite the address like this  http://www.facebook.com@3232283278 . Now it is much more 
difficult to understand this trick. Even some IT professionals cannot recognize easily, unless they are 
very expert to spear-phishing tricks, however you can convert the IP number to integer either by 
your hand or using a website that converts  IP numbers like this one 
https://www.ipaddressguide.com/ip . 
Figure 38: Facebook with google extension

Engineering attack

open the training phishing mail for figure 38 I got the warning on figure 36 and after I ignored that 
warning, again Firefox bowser pups up with one more warning on figure 37, because I already in-

5.2                                     Conclusion 
 
This chapter was a long but important one. We talked about important techniques and tricks used 
by SEs, we started with a small explanation about the SET toolkit which are tools used by cyber 
attackers to hack people, then we moved to talk about SE attack methods including Phishing, 
Pretexting, Dumpster Diving, Quid Pro Quo, Tailgating, Shoulder Surfing and we come up with some 
examples. After that, we explained, what a CEO Fraud is or BEC and how it is used, furthermore, we 
deeply talked about the spear-phishing attacks, conducting a case study that we demonstrated how 
to clone Facebook account using the Site Clone option of SET Toolkit.  After that we make a clear 
example about how multiple spear-phishing attack are performed, how a cyber-attacker masks his 
IP address, the techniques used for @ extension and finally, we introduce some useful browser 
extension such Netcraft that can warn you a phishing mail.

Chapter   Six 
 
 
6    Identifying social engineering risks 
 
 
 
 
One of our goals was to find out how an organization identifies a risk from SE, thus in this chapter 
we are going to talk about risk management process, however to recognize a SE risks, it is important 
to learn and understand what SE is, how dangerous it is for the organizations information-security, 
what the scenarios they use to manipulate their target including the methods we talked in section 
5.1. “SE Attack Methods” in figure 24, then you may avoid or prevent any action that can lead you 
and your employee to fall e-mail scams (Christopher Hadnagy, 2011), however you cannot know 
what the risk and dangerous is, if you do not have any information, training or idea about it. “Teach 
yourself and your employees how to “stop, drop and roll” so to speak, when it comes to these types 
of attacks” (Christopher Hadnagy, 2011), again, people who work for an organization cannot easily 
identify SE tricks unless they have some ideas about what SE methods and techniques, however, 
there are standards and process that organizations use to assess, identify, analyze and evaluate risk, 
we are starting to talk about these processes from the subsequent sections starting with the Risk 
management process. 
 
6.1       Risk Management process 
 
To assess organization’s risks associated with social engineering attacks through publicly available 
information, we implement a process for assessing the probability or likelihood and risk in the 
organization (Pompon, IT Security Risk Control, 2016) 
When we talk about risk management, it is important to quote the international standard ISO 3100 
- risk management, therefore our model below figure 49 is part of the 3100-risk model new version 
in 2018. As we can see in figure 45, a Risk Assessment consists of three subdivisions that we will talk 
in this chapter, however, for complete model of 3100 can be found in appendix B. Because of this 
research limitation, we have only taken the part in the risk management process, which is 
particularly relevant to the scope of our project

Before we start to analyze the model in the above picture, first I conduct an empirical research, 
which we believe is essential for this research. 
 
6.2                           Empirical research 
 
Since I have started writing this thesis even though I read many books related to this research-field 
also I did many literature reviews and reports, despite the fact that I realized that before we begin 
risk assessment, it is necessary to contact with a real-life organization which operating in this 
business environment (has publicly available information) to conduct empirical research, because 
some of the risk assessment factors require answering as a practical perspective in a real-life 
organization, therefore, we have approached with a real organization to inquire about the risk of 
social engineering, about their assets or property, their system, risk model, if any, and other issues 
that are important to the risk assessment. 
After the interview, we can contribute our solution, hypothesis to them. however, to analyze the 
problem correctly, we need to know if they have managed the same before and on which they 
focus on (Pompon, IT Security Risk Control, 2016). We also want to identify any issues with social 
engineering regarding organizations that could harm their system, product, or customer 
Figure 49: Risk Assessment Model 
==PAGE:55==
Risk Analysis for Organizational Information in Relation Social  
Engineering attack   
 
relationship (Pompon, It Security Risk Control Management, 2016). In addition, we will evaluate any 
threats the organization may be exposed to and we will evaluate the likelihood of the threat. 
 
6.2.1                          Choosing empirical research 
 
We choose empirical research because we want to use a qualitative method. The qualitative 
empirical studies can provide rich, deep contextual data to help us understand a phenomenon of 
social engineering (Robert K. Yin, 1994). We also discovered that empirical research can be 
performed based on different ways (Robert K. Yin, 1994), such as a case study, experiment, surveys, 
archive analysis and histories, however, we prefer to use case study interviews because of two 
reasons. 
 
 1. Because of objectiveness of their information in a form of practical experience  
 2. also, for simplicity’s sake. 
 
6.2.1.1                                     Case study 
 
Since we do not have full control over the phenomenon of social engineering risks of publicly 
available organizational information, and we want to answer the question "how and why" of this 
research, however, we answered the question of "how" How public organizational footprint can be 
vulnerable to social engineering attacks? in chapter four, but to answer "why" as a research 
strategy preferred is a case study approach (Robert K. Yin, 1994) for both data collection tactics and 
analysis of the collection. 
As we mentioned before, it is an important and good strategy for us to choose a relevant 
organization in the context being researched. In other words, an organization that operates a 
relevant environment in order to have a link between our investigation-phenomenon and the 
organization's business operational boundary. The main reason we are conducting this case study is 
to create a solid social engineering risk mitigation model after analyzing the risks. 
 
6.2.2                              Context 
 
The basic information for the risk assessment is to gather the scope of the organizations’ assets and 
the resources we want to assess, after identifying that we will analyze all possible threats and 
vulnerabilities to those resources. 
6.2.3                      Chosen Organization type  
 
We have chosen a middle size organization which is an international money transfer organization. 
The interviewee was a stakeholder and the chief of Copenhagen department. He described the 
organization as a very successful organization having many branches in the world, including 
Denmark and all other Scandinavian countries. The organization has four offices in the 4 main 
towns (Copenhagen, Aarhus, Odense, and Aalborg) in Denmark. 
When asked about the organization's assets that are necessary to protect, he described it as 
primarily the consumer data such as customer profiles, employee data, secure payments, and 
==PAGE:56==

Engineering attack   
 
remittances, see the section. The organization does not have a special security team or dedicated 
cyber-security representative or group, but they use an online database system connected the main 
server to transfer money over the world, more information about the organization we will talk in 
section “Organizational Description”  
 
6.2.4                         Organization interview 
 
Before the interview, we have already prepared the questions that we are going to ask the 
representative of the organization to facilitate the interview. This empirical research focuses on an 
interview question, but it is also appreciated any other information that is useful for our research, 
such an organizational document like yearly annual or drafts are also preferable but not 
recommended. The type of interview was as structured depth interview unlikely unstructured one 
(Robert K. Yin, 1994).

measure the impact, threat influence and vulnerability score from these two tables in order to get

6.2.5                                 FAIR 
 
We decide to use FAIR (Open Group, 2010) to analyze the organization’s publicly available 
information against SE risks. There are many other models such as NIST CSF (Rebecca m. Blank, 
2012 and 2018), OCTAVE (Richard A. Caralli, May 2007) and CMS Information Security RA 
Methodology (Group (SSG), 2002), however, FAIR is an analytical risk model that describes what risk 
is, how it works and how to qualify or quantify it, however FAIR also supports qualitative method 
(Pompon, 2016) therefore, we are only using the model to qualify risks. We choose FAIR because it 
suits our needs for the analysis model for the risk. As we can see in figure 50 below, the threat and 
vulnerability are factors of risks and an impact is the consequence of the risk, thus to identify risks, 
firstly, we have to identify the contributed factors, however, before we move to analyze threats and 
vulnerabilities which we identified during the interview, we are going to talk two important tables 
which are: 
==PAGE:59==
Risk Analysis for Organizational Information in Relation Social  
Engineering attack   
 
1- Likelihood table. 
2- Impact table. 
 
 
6.2.5.1                                        RISK 
 
A risk is a matter of future events and is not something that is happening right now. or already 
happened. This unknown future event-occurrence can be estimated by the measure of its 
likelihood-rate, vulnerability and threat. 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
6.2.5.2                                        Likelihood Table 
 
Since we are using a qualitative analysis method it easier for us to use a matrix table, so we created a 
likelihood matrix table 14 (Smock, 2002). We assigned the likelihood of risk occurrence to descriptive 
values or score from least to worst (1-5) to indicate what is the likelihood score that the risk may occur 
to the resources we are going to assess (Pompon, 2016). In our case, social engineering risk to publicly 
available organizational information, thus as we said table 14 represents the likelihood rate of the risk 
occurring which we described as Rare, Unlikely, Possible, Likely, and Almost Certain to occur. After cre-
ating a likelihood table, we also created an impact table in the next section.

6.2.5.3.1 
                     Risk assessment 
 
To assess the risk, we have to analyze first, in addition, before analyzing risks we need to have in mind an 
analytical method (Ayyub, Bilal M., 2014) because an analytical method will consider the likelihood and 
impacts of the existing security level in the organization. As we talked before, there are a couple of 
analytical methods we can use for assessing likelihood and impacts of social engineering. These analytical 
approaches include FAIR, quantitative, Systematic, and qualitative, or semi-quantitative, however, as 
mention before we will use FAIR which also supporting qualitative approach (RADU, 2009) (Robert K. Yin, 
1994). 
 
6.2.5.3.2 
                   Risk Identification 
 
Risk Identification is a process we are going to conduct in order to investigate existing SE risk-factors for 
organization’s publicly available information. This process composed of different steps, including threat 
analysis, vulnerability analysis, risk analysis, risk prioritizing and risk evaluation (Ayyub, Bilal M., 2014). 
From the next section, we are going to begin our risk assessment starting with threat analysis. After 
analysis and evaluating, the results are used then to prioritize individual risks to build a most-to-least-
critical importance ranking system. The risk ranking in terms of their severity or significance provides 
insight into the organization's security management where a risk of social engineering may be requiring 
an urgent response to mitigate for the prioritization of the realization of high likelihood / high impact risk 
events let us start with threat analysis. 
 
6.2.5.4                  Threat analysis 
 
When we are using the FAIR method to analyze risks, it is necessary to find and analyze threat levels, to 
do that we developed threat scenarios (factors) that are relevant for this money transfer organization 
(Pompon, IT Security Risk Control, 2016). The likelihood part of table 16 indicates the level of threats and 
vulnerabilities that can cause risks. The threat factors on table 16 describe which threat actors that can 
cause or contribute to a security incident specific social engineering attacks, these threat factors are 
based on the questions see Appendix B that we asked to the organization's representative in addition his 
answers are in the answer column.

6.2.6                         identifying an existing control  
 
During the evaluation of vulnerabilities, we also determine which controls are already in place. These 
controls include policies or procedures, staff training, responsibilities, reporting structure, security 
equipment, such as firewalls and physical guards or surveillance cameras (Pompon, It Security Risk Control 
Management, 2016). These controls can be strong, medium or weak in the organization's perspective, and 
they can be measured and repeated. Evaluation of existing controls is an important factor for finding 
vulnerabilities and risk mitigation as well. The evaluation of control can be seen in table 15, but now let us 
move to vulnerabilities. 
 
 
6.2.6.1                               VULNERABILITY ANALYSIS 
 
Vulnerability is relating weakness in the organization’s security system, for example, how to protect their as-
sets, the way they are prepared to avoid and the technology they use to protect the property which we 
talked in section” Categorization of organizational assets”.  As we can in table 16, if that weakness exists to 
as high extent that may cause risk-occurrence, consequently the system might be exploited by SE, for exam-
ple in our case, the organization’s data base system might be vulnerable to unauthorized data manipulation, 
because the system does not verify a user's identity before allowing data access, the password is weak to 
guess, or one password may be used for long time in multiple accounts. In other words, vulnerability is de-
termined by the capacity of the organization to mitigate a threat, in addition vulnerability is also understood 
as the factors of risk that might increase or decrease. The next vulnerability matrix table 17 is based on the 
answers of the interviewee from the organization by choosing the rate/score from the prepared likelihood 
table 14.

6.2.6.1.5 
                             Risk Evaluation  
 
When we identified the risk, context, causes, the contributing factors and consequences and properly 
described situations, then we looked at the strengths and weaknesses of organization’s existing 
system and processes designed to help control the risk if any. We also investigated what controls are 
already in place, and if they are effective to help to identify what action is needed, however, we did 
not identify any existing control in the organization which we interviewed and finally, we prioritized 
the risk.  According to our results, the final assessment is that the organization should come up with 
an immediate response to the risks identified after the analysis and investigation either to eliminate 
risk if possible or mitigate them, anyhow let us move to risk treatment. 
 
6.2.6.1.6 
                          Risk Treatment 
 
Since we identified the risk, a quick goal of mitigating risk is to take actions that will drive risk from 
high to a minimum (Smock, 2002). After we identify the risks associated with the organization’s 
publicly accessible information, procedures can be developed to mitigate or control the threats as-
sociated with each risk. Which mitigations are in need to perform for this organization, we talked in 
the next chapter, namely countermeasures and mitigation’s chapter, but for now, let us move to 
risk tolerance.

Engineering attack   
 
 
6.2.6.1.7 
                          Risk tolerance  
 
Risk tolerance is associated with a risk which doesn't have a substantial effect, which can affect the 
day-to-day operations of the organization with regarding the measured risk analysis to determine 
whether the risk is tolerable (Ayyub, Bilal M., 2014). Normally the organization needs to contact 
their insurance broker to implement the policy service needs to estimate risk, however, the risk we 
have analyzed, it is not minor risk instead very high potential risk, therefore we are going to talk 
how to mitigate in chapter 7. 
 
 
6.3                                   Conclusion  
 
This chapter was concerning identifying SE risks, which is one of our goals, however, we started 
with to explain how important is to learn the tricks and techniques of SE then, we presented the 
risk management process, next we talked about the need for to interview an organization and why 
we choose specifically empirical research. After that, we started a case study where we described 
the chosen organization to have an interview with and the type of the organization. Afterward, we 
provided the details of the interviews and we come up with the organizational description as well as 
the categorization of the organization's asset. We talked about the FAIR model and we explained 
why we chose the FAIR, also what could be other alternative methods and suddenly we moved to 
risk description and the relationship between risk, threat, vulnerability, and impact. After that, we 
began to consider the risk assessment, risk identification, threat analysis, identifying current control 
of the organization, then, we performed Vulnerability analysis, risk analysis, risk levels and risk 
matrix graphs. Finally, we concluded to talk about risk prioritizing, risk assessment, risk analysis and 
risk treatment.
